  :                            390 
  :                            391 .	each handle integer corresponds to a 48-bit encoding
  :                            392 .	starting at handle(1)
  :                            393 
  :                            394 .	the index corresponds to a port number or to a
  :                            395 .	TCP connection and is the encoded offset of the
  :                            396 .	control block in the containing bank

  :                            514 .	visible instead of the application

  :                            518 .	whichever it was before

  :                            203 .	_________________________________
  :                            204 .	| socket|   0	|address:port	|
  :                            205 .	|_______|_______|_______________|
  :                            206 
  :                            207 .	note the kernel vector bank is read and
  :                            208 .	written with internal interrupts
  :                            209 
  :                            210 .		ii	READ_XVECTOR
  :                            211 .		ii	WRITE_XVECTOR
  :                            212 
  :                            213 .	because this routine is available
  :                            214 .	both to kernel tasks which share
  :                            215 .	the kernel vector bank, and unkernel
  :                            216 .	tasks which have their own vector
  :                            217 .	bank
  :                            218 
  :                            219 .	the internal ISRs switch to the
  :                            220 .	kernel vector bank
  :                            221 
  :                            222 .		on	see$2
  :                            223 
  :                            224 .	return from any ISR restores all PSR
  :                            225 .	flags to what they were on II
  :                            226 
  :                            227 .	the kernel task which mostly calls here
  :                            228 .	is the IP task in this bank,
  :                            229 .	because some application server protocols
  :                            230 .	are in this task
  :                            231 
  :                            232 .	otherwise the callers are application
  :                            233 .	tasks

  :                            43 .
  :                            44 
  :                            45 . Emulation implements one kind of network interface
  :                            46 
  :                            47 . this can be modified when there is more than one interface type
  :                            48 . initially the only known type is modelled as a dual ported memory
  :                            49 . interface to the system
  :                            50 .
  :                            51 . half of the interfacing memory presents input frames
  :                            52 . the other half is available for output frames
  :                            53 
  :                            54 
  :                            55 .		_________________________________
  :                            56 .	>------>|1st receive buffer		|
  :                            57 .	|	|2nd				|
  :                            58 .	|	|				|
  :                            59 .	^
  :                            60 .	|
  :                            61 .	<-------|last receive buffer		|
  :                            62 .		|_______________________________|
  :                            63 .	>------>|1st transmit buffer		|
  :                            64 .	|	|2nd				|
  :                            65 .	|	|				|
  :                            66 .	^
  :                            67 .	|
  :                            68 .	<-------|last transmit buffer		|
  :                            69 .		|_______________________________|
  :                            70 
  :                            71 
  :                            72 . the frames arrive and are sent in cyclic order of the buffers
  :                            73 .
  :                            74 . there is an 8-word length + ethernet header at the start of each buffer
  :                            75 . this sketch represents a quartet of bits per symbol
  :                            76 
  :                            77 .	 00LLLL 00xxxx 00xxxx 00xxxx 00yyyy 00yyyy 00yyyy 00pppp
  :                            78 
  :                            79 .	________________________________________________________________________
  :                            80 .	| 00LLLL | 00xxxx | 00xxxx | 00xxxx | 00yyyy | 00yyyy | 00yyyy | 00pppp|
  :                            81 .	|________|________|________|________|________|________|________|_______|
  :                            82 .	| 00data | 00....
  :                            83 
  :                            84 
  :                            85 . network data is buffered 16 bits per computer word right up to the socket
  :                            86 . and XDR interfaces. These "interface dual ported" memories may be 16-bit
  :                            87 . memories reading out eight zero bits at the high order positions of each
  :                            88 . computer word, or they may be 24-bit memories: then the high-order 8 bits
  :                            89 . must be maintained with zero values
  :                            90 
  :                            91 .	LLLL	is frame size, and nonzero value written here indicates
  :                            92 .		a complete frame received, or available for transmission
  :                            93 .
  :                            94 .	xxxx xxxx xxxx	is the destination ethernet address
  :                            95 
  :                            96 .	yyyy yyyy yyyy	is the source ethernet address
  :                            97 
  :                            98 .	pppp	is the protocol family indicator, 0800 for IPv4, 0806 for ARP
  :                            99 .		as many as required
  :                            100 
  :                            101 
  :                            102 . the buffers are equal sized and used cyclically
  :                            103 
  :                            104 . the input machine signals to software that a frame is available
  :                            105 . by writing a length in bits 15:0 of the first word in the buffer:
  :                            106 .
  :                            107 .		0000 0000 LLLL LLLL LLLL LLLL
  :                            108 .
  :                            109 . The length is not self-inclusive. It is the frame length.
  :                            110 . The frame starts at the next word
  :                            111 
  :                            112 . software indicates that the frame has been sampled and the buffer is
  :                            113 . free by writing zero to this length word
  :                            114 
  :                            115 . software indicates that a frame for transmission is assembled 
  :                            116 . in the transmission buffer by writing a length in bits 15:0 of
  :                            117 . the first word in the transmission buffer. The length is the
  :                            118 . frame length and not self-inclusive
  :                            119 
  :                            120 . The frame for transmission starts in the second word of the
  :                            121 . transmission buffer
  :                            122 
  :                            123 . Transmission completion is indicated back to the software by zero
  :                            124 . in the length word, and this also indicates the transmission buffer
  :                            125 . is again free
  :                            126 
  :                            127 . This interface model is not part of RTA architecture but supports
  :                            128 . this emulation and is intended to work like network interfaces are 
  :                            129 . intended for RTA systems
  :                            130 . 
  :                            131 . That is, some of the 63 DEVICEs (arrays outside system memory)
  :                            132 . should carry network data to and from an RTA system.
  :                            133 
  :                            134 . A DEVICE is up to 65536 BANKS of up to 262144 WORDS of up to 24 bits
  :                            135 .
  :                            136 
  :                            137 . device 0 is system memory or executable space and is up to 16 gigawords
  :                            138 . of 24-bit memory
  :                            139 
  :                            140 . only system memory is selectable in pages of 4096 words as well as in
  :                            141 . banks of 256K
  :                            142 
  :                            143 . Memory in the other 63 possible devices is selectable in banks of
  :                            144 . 262144 words only
  :                            145 
  :                            146 . the pointer to a page of system memory based in one of the 72 relocation
  :                            147 . registers has the layout
  :                            148 
  :                            149 .	00pp pppp pppp pppp pppp pppp
  :                            150 
  :                            151 . where bit 22 = 0 indicates a 22-bit page pointer and the task has a granule
  :                            152 . of 4096 words addressed and available
  :                            153 
  :                            154 . the pointer to a bank of system memory in a relocation register has the layout
  :                            155 
  :                            156 .	01pp pppp pppp pppp pp00 0000
  :                            157 
  :                            158 . where bit 22 = 1 indicates a 16-bit bank pointer and the task has a granule
  :                            159 . of 262144 words addressed and available
  :                            160 
  :                            161 . bits 5:0 = 00 0000 indicate that the bank is in system memory
  :                            162 .
  :                            163 . the pointer to a bank of "I/O Memory" (strictly an array other than
  :                            164 . system memory) has the layout in a relocation register
  :                            165 
  :                            166 .	01bb bbbb bbbb bbbb bbDE VICE
  :                            167 
  :                            168 . where bit 22 = 1 = Big Bank ** DEVICE = nonzero = some other device than
  :                            169 . system memory
  :                            170 
  :                            171 . Some of these 63 possible arrays outside system memory will be battery-
  :                            172 . supported SRAMS of 24-bit words containing realtime transaction file systems
  :                            173 
  :                            174 . Others may be flash memories containing default system images and occasional
  :                            175 . data backups (but flash memories may not be used for high-frequency
  :                            176 . transaction update data)
  :                            177 
  :                            178 . some may be insertable flashes containing 16- or 8-bit memories organised
  :                            179 . in a file system architecture readable and writable by other systems
  :                            180 
  :                            181 . some may be video RAMs of up to 16 Gigawords of 24-bit words
  :                            182 
  :                            183 . At least one device must be an array interfacing a network adaptor to the
  :                            184 . RTA system with 16 network data bits per computer word as modelled here
  :                            185 
  :                            186 . This interface array is supplied to the emulator by a Unix shared memory
  :                            187 . block of 524288 bytes which looks to RTA like a device of one bank of
  :                            188 . 262144 16-bit words.
  :                            189 
  :                            190 . RTA reads each word out as a 24-bit computer word with bits 23:16 = 0000 0000
  :                            191 . The first half of this block is receive buffers and the second half transmit
  :                            192 . buffers
  :                            193 
  :                            194 . the settings here are that RPAGEW is 1024 words. This is the buffer size
  :                            195 . and it allows an ethernet frame of 1514+ bytes. Jumbos are not accounted
  :                            196 . for in the emulation. A device in a large-configuration RTA may have
  :                            197 . 65536 * 262144 words in a network interface device, but the emulator has
  :                            198 . only 1 * 262144 words in its one network interface space.
  :                            199 
  :                            200 . Therefore the emulator will only test standard ethernet traffic
  :                            201 
  :                            202 . the configuration of the emulated interface (which links to a real interface
  :                            203 . outside the emulator) is therefore
  :                            204 
  :                            205 .	128 receive buffers of 1024 words each
  :                            206 . plus	128 transmit buffers of 1024 words each
  :                            207 
  :                            208 
  :                            209 . Notes. Note 1
  :                            210 
  :                            211 . To avoid accidental damage, device banks (as distinct from system memory
  :                            212 . banks) may only be based by interrupt code. This avoids inadvertant
  :                            213 . traversal of array accesses across all the devices. At its simplest,
  :                            214 . the internal interrupts (system calls II 64+32 .. II 64+63) only need
  :                            215 . to copy the bank handle from user-A register, base it, and return:
  :                            216 
  :                            217 .		la	A
  :                            218 .		sabr	32
  :                            219 .		ir	0
  :                            220 
  :                            221 		.
  :                            222 		.
  :                            223 
  :                            224 .		la	A
  :                            225 .		sabr	63
  :                            226 .		ir	0
  :                            227 
  :                            228 . but as much extra security can be added to that as wished in more complex OS
  :                            229 
  :                            230 . Once the storage area is based, the application can access it at will.
  :                            231 . If this is not wished, the interrupt code itself must carry out required
  :                            232 . operations and unbase the storage area before return. The simplest model
  :                            233 . here relies on trusted applications which base the sensitive banks
  :                            234 . by design and not by accident
  :                            235 
  :                            236 . This also provides the beginning of a security model for any implementation
  :                            237 . which includes the translatable storage handle
  :                            238 
  :                            239 . Where a handle 0xxx xxxx xxxx xxxx xxxx is absolute whether it points
  :                            240 . to system memory or a device
  :                            241 
  :                            242 . 1xxx xxxx xxxx xxxx xxxx is reserved for a possible translating range of
  :                            243 . storage area handles, for moveable/swappable application storage.
  :                            244 . A hardware implementation which does translatable handles would reserve
  :                            245 . the basing of all absolute areas (device and system memory) to privileged
  :                            246 . code of some sort, perhaps system calls as in this initial model
  :                            247 
  :                            248 . applications could then only directly base an allowed set of areas described
  :                            249 . in their translate lists.
  :                            250 
  :                            251 
  :                            252 
  :                            253 . Note 2
  :                            254 
  :                            255 . the interface handler here will offer each frame to the protocol family
  :                            256 . (mostly IPv4 or similar), which must decide immediately whether to take
  :                            257 . the frame and place it in storage visible to the application.
  :                            258 
  :                            259 . This means IP will check IP correctness, and call ICMP, UDP or TCP which
  :                            260 . must checksum as necessary while copying the datagram to socket storage.
  :                            261 
  :                            262 . This is not a flat-address system and there is no zero-copy
  :                            263 . receive. But there is only one copy, and it is carried out during checksum
  :                            264 . generation. After that the datagrams are in socket-associated buffers which
  :                            265 . are placed in the address space of the application.
  :                            266 
  :                            267 . The socket or XDR interface will format the data when the application
  :                            268 . subsequently receives it over the socket API.
  :                            269 
  :                            270 . on return from the call to the protocol family, the interface buffer is
  :                            271 . freed
  :                            272 
  :                            273 
  :                            274 
  :                            275 RX_LL_HL $equ	d$save
  :                            276 
  :                            277 
  :                            278 
  :                            279 $(1)
  :                            280 
  :                            281 .	pointer array to the network interface descriptors
  :                            282 .	these are numbered starting from one as in MIB-II
  :                            283 .	interfaces table
  :                            284 
  :                            285 .	word zero of this table is a count of the number of
  :                            286 .	interface descriptors in use
  :                            287 
  :                            288 .	the initial value 1 indicates that there is one
  :                            289 .	interface so far, interface(1) = internal loopback,
  :                            290 .	and the next to be configured ought to be interface(2)
  :                            291 
  :                            292 .	to obtain a descriptor for a new interface, increment
  :                            293 .	the counter in the first word of the array and select
  :                            294 .	the pointer at
  :                            295 
  :                            296 .	i_f_pointer_array[i_f_pointer_array[0]]
  :                            297 
  :                            298 .	for example
  :                            299 .
  :                            300 .		lx		i_f_pointer_array
  :                            301 .		ax, xi		1
  :                            302 .		sx		i_f_pointer_array
  :                            303 .		lx		0, x
  :                            304

  :                            46 HEADER_LONGWORDS $equ	d$save1	. 17 used in receive
  :                            47 TP_HEADER	$equ	d$save2	. 18 used in receive and transmit
  :                            48 I_F_IDX		$equ	d$save3	. 19 used in receive
  :                            49 
  :                            50 TX_AXW		$equ	8	. used in transmit
  :                            51 TX_FRAME	$equ	9	. used in receive and transmit
  :                            52 
  :                            53 HEADER_BYTES	$equ	d$save	. 16 used in transmit
  :                            54 MTU		$equ	d$save1	. 17 used in transmit
  :                            55 I_F		$equ	d$save2	. 19 used in transmit
  :                            56 
  :                            57 TX_FLAG_OFFSET	$equ	10	. used in tranmsit
  :                            58 TX_DATAGRAML	$equ	11	. used in transmit = unfragmented datagram
  :                            59 				. generated in interfaces = xmitted frame
  :                            60 
  :                            61 DYNAMIC_IP_HEADER $equ	0
  :                            62 		. if IP header length cannot be known at socket layer
  :                            63 		. then make this a multiple of 4 > 20 and < 64
  :                            64 		. to give a fixed large space for IP to add header
  :                            65 		. options
  :                            66 
  :                            67 		. otherwise IP header size is fixed when the socket
  :                            68 		. layer acquires a buffer

  :                            107 
  :                            108 .	this is the direct feed to udpq from interfaces which have
  :                            109 .	fixed dual-ported buffering. Interfaces with a direct access
  :                            110 .	to system memory would not have this packet copy
  :                            111 
  :                            112 .	The packet copy is used as an occasion to checksum
  :                            113 
  :                            114 .	The packet copy places the packets in rx_buffer with
  :                            115 .	T77 descriptors which are called ACWs
  :                            116 
  :                            117 .	This routine cannot be called for fragmented datagrams
  :                            118 .	because it is instant from frame readout to UDP checksum
  :                            119 .	generation
  :                            120 
  :                            121 .	this is an upward call from ipv4_rx

(+01)10
00:00046F 5D02E7              +175 	ly, xi	udpq
00:000470 60000E              +176 	la	fp
00:000471 3E0C98              +177 	lcal	q2
  :                            178 	
00:000472 B6032F              +179 	j	ipv4_rxz
  :                            180 
  :                            181 
  :                            182 .	this is the feed to udpq for reassembled datagrams
  :                            183 .	which are already in a chain of rx_buffer with ACW
  :                            184 .	descriptors
  :                            185 
  :                            186 .	each fragment has the checksum addition of its data
  :                            187 .	in the ACW, to exploit the chance of checksum
  :                            188 .	generation during copy to rx_buffer
  :                            189 
  :                            190 .	it has been second-guessed in all cases that checksum
  :                            191 .	is specified in the UDP header (the UDP header may not
  :                            192 .	be the first fragment of the datagram to arrive).
  :                            193 
  :                            194 .	also, it is unlikely that UDP payload large enough
  :                            195 .	to need reassembly would be exempt from checksum
  :                            196 
  :                            197 .	no-checksum is usually a feature of high-speed
  :                            198 .	media traffic which is transmitted in much smaller
  :                            199 .	packets than any MTU
  :                            200 
  :                            201 .	large reassembly is more likely to be traffic like
  :                            202 .	NFS in 8KByte slabs (or 3KByte slabs where RTA is the
  :                            203 .	NFS client). Checksum would certainly apply
  :                            204 
  :                            205 .	So all the data arriving here has been end-around
  :                            206 .	added and the positive sum left in the ACW
  :                            207 
  :                            208 .	if the UDP header has a nonzero checksum, the data sums
  :                            209 .	are now summed with network address, protocol index
  :                            210 .	and user datagram length, then inverted and if finally
  :                            211 .	zero inverted again. Then compared.
  :                            212

  :                            249 udp_txbx
00:000483 3D0000              +250 	fret	0
  :                            251 				. this is a far call because
  :                            252 				. socket users may call it
  :                            253 
  :                            254 				. unlike the similar calls
  :                            255 				. in icmp and tcp which are
  :                            256 				. not application visible
  :                            257 
  :                            258 
  :                            259 
  :                            260 . far calls
  :                            261 
  :                            262 .	udp_acw_send
  :                            263 .	udp_acw_send_nocheck
  :                            264 
  :                            265 . get to here
  :                            266 
  :                            267 . udp_txaw
  :                            268 . udp_txaw_nocheck
  :                            269 
  :                            270 . low level call with the acw adress in X
  :                            271 
  :                            272 . both ports must be in the UDP header
  :                            273 . both adresses must be in the address buffer
  :                            274 
  :                            275 . if there is no come-from address in the address buffer
  :                            276 . the thing cannot now be checksummed and an earlier
  :                            277 . call should have done the internal routing step to
  :                            278 . find the sending interface and its default network
  :                            279 . address
  :                            280 
  :                            281 . so in that case an error gets returned as described
  :                            282 . in the rest of this discussion
  :                            283 
  :                            284 . it's the interface number that is important in
  :                            285 . finding a local address for the datagram and
  :                            286 . for the checksum
  :                            287 
  :                            288 . it's also optimal if the interface index is already
  :                            289 . in the descriptor
  :                            290 
  :                            291 . we have the local address and the interface number
  :                            292 . already when we just bounce back reversing the
  :                            293 . incoming addresses as a server with the calls to
  :                            294 
  :                            295 . 	udp_rtxbuffer->udp_response_buffer
  :                            296 
  :                            297 . and after message construction, to here:
  :                            298 
  :                            299 .	udp_acw_send->udp_txaw
  :                            300 
  :                            301 . some implementations suggest you do a connect
  :                            302 . which makes a routing call to IP and plugs the
  :                            303 . default network address of the interface back
  :                            304 . into the socket
  :                            305 
  :                            306 . having a from address in the socket is
  :                            307 . usable when you are making the conventional
  :                            308 . calls send and sendto
  :                            309 
  :                            310 . the connect idea is nice for send but not
  :                            311 . for sendto which is meant for random desinations
  :                            312 . reached over various interfaces
  :                            313 
  :                            314 . it's also not right that an application should
  :                            315 . need to pick a local network address before
  :                            316 . binding a UDP socket. The application and its
  :                            317 . developer cannot know that unless the
  :                            318 . application is for one host with one interface
  :                            319 
  :                            320 . since this is the final path step of all the
  :                            321 . possible interface calls to send UDP
  :                            322 
  :                            323 . and the step where checksum is done if opted
  :                            324 . and the come-from address therefore needed
  :                            325 
  :                            326 . we'll do the following here
  :                            327 
  :                            328 . we'll return with an error code if there is
  :                            329 . no source address, because
  :                            330 
  :                            331 . if you got here with the primitive
  :                            332 
  :                            333 .	udp_acw_send
  :                            334 
  :                            335 . then it was up to you to copy the address
  :                            336 . information into the buffers. At this
  :                            337 . point it's no longer certain that the
  :                            338 . socket/port structure is based. That's
  :                            339 . not even in the parameters to this call
  :                            340 . and it's intended that the caller decides
  :                            341 . what at this point is based in socket
  :                            342 . space
  :                            343 
  :                            344 . so we'll send back an error
  :                            345 
  :                            346 . if you obtained the buffer with
  :                            347 
  :                            348 .	udp_rtxbuffer
  :                            349 
  :                            350 . then that DID set the addresses up for you
  :                            351 
  :                            352 . if you came from send, send had access
  :                            353 . to your socket and copied the addresses
  :                            354 . in there
  :                            355 
  :                            356 . if you then get to here with no come-from
  :                            357 . address you hadn't done the connect call
  :                            358 . which writes both to and from addresses
  :                            359 . to the socket. So the same error return
  :                            360 . is appropriate, we'll call it something
  :                            361 . like
  :                            362 
  :                            363 . UDP_NOSOURCENET	$equ	-5
  :                            364 
  :                            365 . if you got here from sendto, that makes
  :                            366 . the same internal routing call as connect
  :                            367 . for every datagram
  :                            368 . but writes what it finds to the datagram
  :                            369 . address buffer and not to the socket
  :                            370 
  :                            371 . recvfrom can cheekily write all the
  :                            372 . incoming addressing to the socket
  :                            373 
  :                            374 . then sendto can skip the internal routing
  :                            375 . step if the socket has a source address
  :                            376 . and the destinaton address stored on the
  :                            377 . socket is the sendto parameter address
  :                            378 
  :                            379 . send can just send on the recvfrom
  :                            380 . addresses as if it had connected
  :                            381 
  :                            382 . since the routing call finds the next hop
  :                            383 . as well as the interface, that information
  :                            384 . stays in the address buffer of the datagram
  :                            385 . and IP routing will not need to find the
  :                            386 . next hop again for this one datagram
  :                            387 
  :                            388 . for connect/send the next-hop isn't saved,
  :                            389 . because it might change. But the interface
  :                            390 . is saved
  :                            391

  :                            185 	.		window allows

  :                            196 
  :                            197 	.	5:	if more but sequence + length
  :                            198 	.		within local ACK + window, deferq
  :                            199 	.		and otherwise drop
  :                            200 
  :                            201 	.	6:	accept as much of the net input at 4:
  :                            202 	.		as local window allowed. This avoids
  :                            203 	.		accepting single bytes which are
  :                            204 	.		sent to push the window open
  :                            205 	.		and also avoids rejecting payload
  :                            206 	.		for which we have advertised
  :                            207 	.		window. Drop the segment if no
  :                            208 	.		part of it is accepted, and shave
  :                            209 	.		the trailing edge if part of it
  :                            210 	.		is rejected
  :                            211 
  :                            212 	.		It might be unwise to accept beyond
  :                            213 	.		window. A well behaved remote system
  :                            214 	.		won't send beyond window (apart from
  :                            215 	.		experimental 1-byters, and the response
  :                            216 	.		to those is drop them and advertise
  :                            217 	.		new window). A malicious remote system
  :                            218 	.		might try to manipulate window
  :                            219 
  :                            220 	.	7:	Advance local ACK and reduce local
  :                            221 	.		window according to the amount accepted
  :                            222 
  :                            223 	.	8:	generate new local window
  :                            224 
  :                            225 	.	9:	transmit segments queued for remote window
  :                            226 	.		according to remote ACK + window,  and
  :                            227 	.		if there is none, and no further input
  :                            228 	.		segment after this has yet been received
  :                            229 	.		for this connection, send an ACK, unless
  :                            230 	.		the incoming segment is an ACK without
  :                            231 	.		payload and its SEQ is not beyond local ACK
  :                            232 
  :                            233 	.		SYN and FIN have a payload weight of
  :                            234 	.		1 byte
  :                            235 
  :                            236 	.		restart connection:timer(1)
  :                            237 	.		maybe do this at an early point, depending
  :                            238 	.		whether the attempt to communicate
  :                            239 	.		counts as keepalive even if it is
  :                            240 	.		out of range
  :                            241 
  :                            242 
  :                            243 
  :                            244 
  :                            245 	.	1	is the remote ACK possible?

  :                            1749 
  :                            1750 .	buffer queues which are shared between tasks have to be accessed
  :                            1751 .	in a protected way. They are mainly the queues which both the IP
  :                            1752 .	task and an application protocol access. Some application protocols
  :                            1753 .	are in the IP task and some are not
  :                            1754 
  :                            1755 .	it would be quite bad to place TS cells in every queue structure
  :                            1756 .	because not all queues need them. Sockets and connections are the
  :                            1757 .	best available place for these lock cells
  :                            1758 
  :                            1759 .	all this code is SMP, so the code
  :                            1760 
  :                            1761 .	1.	prevents itself from getting interrupted or switched
  :                            1762 .	2.	locks the connection
  :                            1763 .	3.	updates the queue
  :                            1764 .	4.	frees the lock
  :                            1765 .	5.	reloads the interrupt mask it had before
  :                            1766 
  :                            1767 
  :                            1768 .	tcp_write_rxq is called from TCP in the IP task

  :                            46 
  :                            47 .	these blocking-only calls are central to the
  :                            48 .	known classical POSIX-like socket interface
  :                            49 
  :                            50 .	they correspond to the vectors	listen()
  :                            51 .					accept()
  :                            52 .					close()
  :                            53 .					connect()
  :                            54 
  :                            55 .	their blocking characteristic is in this file connect.msm
  :                            56 
  :                            57 .	and they complement		recv()
  :                            58 .	and				send()
  :                            59 
  :                            60 .	which block or not per flags argument
  :                            61 
  :                            62 .	first parameter of these routines is the handle integer
  :                            63 
  :                            64 .	the target connections of these routines are
  :                            65 .	in the default connections array
  :                            66 .	not the high-volume connection arrays
  :                            67

  :                            95 
  :                            96 .	for write to socket buffer
  :                            97 .	16 bits at a time are read from user data
  :                            98 .	via the staging register
  :                            99 
  :                            100 .	PSR designator b$streamZR is on
  :                            101 .	b$stream16r gets cleared here
  :                            102 .	this is for 24-bit opaque data
  :                            103 .	
  :                            104 
  :                            105 .	in TCP only
  :                            106 .	the caller may write an odd number of bytes
  :                            107 .	into the transmit segment buffer, so there
  :                            108 .	may be a data word half written from the 
  :                            109 .	previous send call
  :                            110 
  :                            111 .	the pointer is apdata[word] + bytes[byte]
  :                            112 .	descriptor:bytes is updated
  :                            113 
  :                            114 .	the segment may be filled and queued, and
  :                            115 .	a new segment started
  :                            116 
  :                            117 .	on calling, the connection is in x
  :                            118 .	the caller may have a dynamic frame on FP
  :                            119 .	we save fp and use it as descriptor:
  :                            120 
  :                            121 .	the user data is in q:RDATA:RDATAC
  :                            122 .	the user byte count is in a

00:000CA6 28B000              +250 	sb	queue:head
00:000CA7 350000              +251 readqz	lret	0
  :                            252 
  :                            253         $if      QSIMULTANEOUS
  :                            254 
  :                            255 .	these use the i_f index word as a next pointer
  :                            256 .	instead of the next pointer at the front of the
  :                            257 .	descriptor
  :                            258 
  :                            259 .	this is so that TCP segments awaiting ACK
  :                            260 .	can be on the interface queue and the
  :                            261 .	connection's ACK wait queue at the same time
  :                            262 
  :                            263 .	the bytes count in the descriptor has
  :                            264 .	bit 23 on to instruct the interface not
  :                            265 .	to drop the buffer after it is sent
  :                            266 
  :                            267 .	in the case of high volume banks where a
  :                            268 .	collection of TCP connections shares a
  :                            269 .	switched output bank at B80000 for segments
  :                            270 .	awaiting first window then ACK, separate
  :                            271 .	from the fixed transmit buffers at F00000
  :                            272 
  :                            273 .	where the two owners of the buffer are
  :                            274 .	the socket and the interface, it is the
  :                            275 .	interface which uses the next_bdi pointer
  :                            276 .	in the descriptor
  :                            277 
  :                            278 .	when actions are carried out on the socket
  :                            279 .	its transmit buffer bank at B80000 is always
  :                            280 .	based. This is only an issue of high volume
  :                            281 .	transaction connections which are intended to
  :                            282 .	exist in very large numbers and therefore
  :                            283 .	cannot keep all their transmitted segments
  :                            284 .	in the fixed  megaword at F00000 until remote
  :                            285 .	ACK arrives from the network
  :                            286 
00:000CA8 70B000              +287 q2if	tz	queue:head
00:000CA9 B60CAE              +288 	j	q2ifz
00:000CAA 20B000              +289 	sa	queue:head
00:000CAB 20B001              +290 	sa	queue:tail

00:000CAD 350000              +292 	lret	0

  :                            419 .	when you first store a managed object

  :                            423 .	when you subsequently store the same

  :                            427

  :                            469 
00:000363 D50004              +470 	ana, xi	4
00:000364 7E036A              +471 	jc	mib_store_long_range?
00:000365 B60370              +472 	j	mib_store_range?
  :                            473 
  :                            474 .	the method of having signed and unsigned range checks
  :                            475 .	without noticing too much
  :                            476 
  :                            477 .	firstly, if the high-order bit of the upper and lower limit
  :                            478 .	are identical, just do unsigned compares, and that will give
  :                            479 .	the right result whether the candidate integer is meant to
  :                            480 .	be signed or not
  :                            481 
  :                            482 .	secondly, if the high order bits of the two limits are 
  :                            483 .	different, then
  :                            484 
  :                            485 .	if the upper limit interpreted as unsigned is greater than
  :                            486 .	the lower limit interpreted as unsigned (the high order bit
  :                            487 .	of the upper limit is on, and not the high order bit of the
  :                            488 .	lower limit), just do an unsigned compare, because the
  :                            489 .	low-high difference cannot be a negative difference between
  :                            490 .	two signed integers. It can only be a lower limit in the
  :                            491 .	lower half of the number space, and an upper limit in the
  :                            492 .	upper half of the unsigned number space
  :                            493 
  :                            494 .	in all the above cases, the candidate integer is within
  :                            495 .	the range tested, if both carry is generated from the
  :                            496 .	subtraction of the lower limit from the integer, and also
  :                            497 .	carry is generated from the subtraction of the integer from
  :                            498 .	the upper limit
  :                            499 
  :                            500 .	this depends upon carry being consistent with adding the
  :                            501 .	minus of one number to the plus of another
  :                            502 
  :                            503 .	RTA1 is logically correct for this. The result and carried
  :                            504 .	result of
  :                            505 
  :                            506 .		ana	THIS
  :                            507 .		anb	THAT
  :                            508 .		dan	THE_OTHER
  :                            509 
  :                            510 
  :                            511 .	are identical to the result and carried result of
  :                            512 
  :                            513 .		aa	MINUS_THIS
  :                            514 .		ab	MINUS_THAT
  :                            515 .		da	MINUS_THE_OTHER
  :                            516 
  :                            517 .	carry$ set after ana anb dan means the unsigned number in
  :                            518 .	the accumulators was at least as large as the number
  :                            519 .	subtracted from it
  :                            520 .		
  :                            521 .
  :                            522 .	if the high-order bit of the lower limit is on and not the
  :                            523 .	high order bit of the upper limit, this is a signed range
  :                            524 .	which includes the value zero between its upper and lower
  :                            525 .	bounds. Then the candidate integer is within the range
  :                            526 .	tested if both the result of the integer minus the lower limit
  :                            527 .	is of positive polarity, and also the result of the upper limit
  :                            528 .	minus the integer is positive polarity
  :                            529 
  :                            530 .	polarity (and zero) are tested in the registers holding the
  :                            531 .	results, because the only condition flag in RTA1 is carry$
  :                            532 
  :                            533 .	examples of instructions testing results other than carry are
  :                            534 
  :                            535 .		jpa	here
  :                            536 .		jnb	there
  :                            537 .		jdz	everywhere
  :                            538 .		jzb	b_is_zero
  :                            539 .		jpe	a_has_even_parity
  :                            540 
  :                            541 .		tp	location_a_few_million
  :                            542 .		j	the_word_at_location_a_few_million_is_negative
  :                            543 
  :                            544 .		tz	2, sp
  :                            545 .		j	the_third_register_in_the_internal_stack_is_nonzero
  :                            546 
  :                            547 
  :                            548 mib_store_long_range!

00:000537 5D205A              +128 	ly, xi	a2_ber_transient(3)
00:000538 3E0700              +129 	lcal	ber_rs
  :                            130 
  :                            131 
  :                            132 
  :                            133 .	the parts to pull out are
  :                            134 .	_________________________
  :                            135 
  :                            136 .	30LL	binding and length		00..7F 81:80..FF 82:0100..FFFF
  :                            137 .	0201vv	SNMP version	00 = v 1
  :                            138 
  :                            139 .	then for v 1 at least...
  :                            140 
  :                            141 .	04LLalpha community string
  :                            142 
  :                            143 .	AxLL	command byte pdu length		00..7F 81:80..FF 82:0100..FFFF
  :                            144 .	160 = get 161 = getnext 163 = set
  :                            145 
  :                            146 .	0204xxxxxxxx	request ID typically 4 bytes
  :                            147 
  :                            148 .	020100	status
  :                            149 .	020100	error index
  :                            150 
  :                            151 .	30LL	binding and length		00..7F 81:80..FF 82:0100..FFFF
  :                            152 .	30LL-2	maybe another one nested
  :                            153 
  :                            154 .	06LLxxxxxx...0500	oids with NULLtype zeroL for get / getnext
  :                            155 .	06LLxxxxxx...4103yyyyyy	oids with typeLL / value for set
  :                            156 
  :                            157 .	strings may be > 127 bytes, length is encoded
  :                            158 .						00..7F 81:80..FF 82:0100..FFFF
  :                            159 
  :                            160 .	______________________________________________________________
  :                            161 
  :                            162 .	if the command is not A0 A1 A3 the request is dropped silently
  :                            163 
  :                            164 .	if the bindings lengths don't figure with the containing lengths
  :                            165 .	the request is dropped silently
  :                            166 
  :                            167 .	if community string is unknown the request is dropped silently
  :                            168 
  :                            169 
  :                            170 .	the response is as the request except
  :                            171 .	_____________________________________
  :                            172 
  :                            173 .	the command byte or response is SNMP_RESPONSE A2 162
  :                            174 
  :                            175 .	the response to successful get / getnext contains
  :                            176 .	a value encoding, not NULLtype/zeroL 0500
  :                            177 
  :                            178 
  :                            179 .	if anything has went wrong
  :                            180 
  :                            181 .		status is the error
  :                            182 
  :                            183 .		1 = something was too large in some way		tooBig
  :                            184 .		2 = that does not exist				noSuchName
  :                            185 .		3 = value wrong type or out of range		badValue
  :                            186 .		4 = object may not be wrote			readOnly
  :                            187 .		5 = something else was wrong			genErr
  :                            188 
  :                            189 .		error index indicates the [1st] oid binding which is in error
  :                            190 .		relative 1, 1 = 1st oid binding in the PDU
  :                            191 
  :                            192 .	the plan is
  :                            193 .	take all the actions and table the results as BER templates
  :                            194 
  :                            195 .	if anything is wrong, send the bindings back as received
  :                            196 .	with error-code set to the mistake
  :                            197 .	and error-index pointing to the first binding which is in error
  :                            198 
  :                            199 .	(no further ones need be scanned if that happens)
  :                            200 .	this means the update values are returned but not actioned
  :                            201 
  :                            202 .	and the read-requests are returned with null values as received
  :                            203 
  :                            204 .	even multiple get-nexts can be sensibly answered on this principle
  :                            205 
  :                            206 .	and if everything has went right, produce the BER-encoded results
  :                            207 
  :                            208
